<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Æthelstan</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<p><b>Æthelstan</b> or <b>Athelstan</b> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>: <i>Æþelstan</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup>, <i>Æðelstān</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup>, meaning "noble stone"; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 894 – 27 October 939) was <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_of_Wessex" title="List of monarchs of Wessex">King of the Anglo-Saxons</a> from 924 to 927 and <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">King of the English</a> from 927 to 939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[c]</a></sup> He was the son of King <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Elder" title="Edward the Elder">Edward the Elder</a> and his first wife, <a href="/wiki/Ecgwynn" title="Ecgwynn">Ecgwynn</a>. Modern historians regard him as the first King of England and one of the greatest Anglo-Saxon kings. He never married, and was succeeded by his half-brother, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_I" title="Edmund I">Edmund</a>.</p>
<p>When Edward died in July 924, Æthelstan was accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercians</a> as king. His half-brother <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfweard_of_Wessex" title="Ælfweard of Wessex">Ælfweard</a> may have been recognised as king in <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, but died within three weeks of their father's death. Æthelstan still encountered resistance in Wessex for several months, and was not crowned until September 925. In 927 he conquered the last remaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Viking</a> kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_York" title="Scandinavian York">York</a>, making him the first Anglo-Saxon ruler of the whole of England. In 934 he invaded Scotland and forced <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Constantine II</a> to submit to him, but Æthelstan's rule was resented by the Scots and Vikings, and in 937 they invaded England. Æthelstan defeated them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a>, a victory which gave him great prestige both in the British Isles and on the Continent. After his death in 939 the Vikings seized back control of York, and it was not finally reconquered until 954.</p>
<p>Æthelstan centralised government; he increased control over the production of <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charters</a> and summoned leading figures from distant areas to his councils. These meetings were also attended by rulers from outside his territory, especially Welsh kings, who thus acknowledged his overlordship. More legal texts survive from his reign than from any other 10th-century English king. They show his concern about widespread robberies, and the threat they posed to social order. His legal reforms built on those of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>. Æthelstan was one of the most pious West Saxon kings, and was known for collecting relics and founding churches. His household was the centre of English learning during his reign, and it laid the foundation for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictine" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> monastic reform later in the century. No other West Saxon king played as important a role in European politics as Æthelstan, and he arranged the marriages of several of his sisters to continental rulers.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>By the ninth century the many kingdoms of the early Anglo-Saxon period had been consolidated into four: Wessex, Mercia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northumbria" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_East_Anglia" title="Kingdom of East Anglia">East Anglia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[4]</a></sup> In the eighth century, Mercia had been the most powerful kingdom in southern England, but in the early ninth, Wessex became dominant under Æthelstan's great-great-grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Egbert_of_Wessex" title="Egbert of Wessex">Egbert</a>. In the middle of the century, England came under increasing attack from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Viking</a> raids, culminating in invasion by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Heathen_Army" title="Great Heathen Army">Great Heathen Army</a> in 865. By 878, the Vikings had overrun East Anglia, Northumbria, and Mercia, and nearly conquered Wessex. The West Saxons fought back under Alfred the Great, and achieved a decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edington" title="Battle of Edington">Battle of Edington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[5]</a></sup> Alfred and the Viking leader <a href="/wiki/Guthrum" title="Guthrum">Guthrum</a> agreed on a division that gave Alfred western Mercia, while eastern Mercia was incorporated into Viking East Anglia. In the 890s, renewed Viking attacks were successfully fought off by Alfred, assisted by his son (and Æthelstan's father) Edward and <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred,_Lord_of_the_Mercians" title="Æthelred, Lord of the Mercians">Æthelred, Lord of the Mercians</a>. Æthelred ruled English Mercia under Alfred and was married to his daughter <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfl%C3%A6d" title="Æthelflæd">Æthelflæd</a>. Alfred died in 899 and was succeeded by Edward. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwold_of_Wessex" title="Æthelwold of Wessex">Æthelwold</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_of_Wessex" title="Æthelred of Wessex">Æthelred</a>, King Alfred's older brother and predecessor as king, made a bid for power, but was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Holme" title="Battle of the Holme">Battle of the Holme</a> in 902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Little is known of warfare between the English and the Danes over the next few years, but in 909, Edward sent a West Saxon and Mercian army to ravage Northumbria. The following year the Northumbrian Danes attacked Mercia, but suffered a decisive defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tettenhall" title="Battle of Tettenhall">Battle of Tettenhall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerEdward_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-MillerEdward-10">[7]</a></sup> Æthelred died in 911 and was succeeded as ruler of Mercia by his widow Æthelflæd. Over the next decade Edward and Æthelflæd conquered Viking Mercia and East Anglia. Æthelflæd died in 918 and was briefly succeeded by her daughter <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfwynn" title="Ælfwynn">Ælfwynn</a>, but in the same year Edward deposed her and took direct control of Mercia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Edward died in 924, he controlled all of England south of the <a href="/wiki/Humber" title="Humber">Humber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerEdward_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-MillerEdward-10">[7]</a></sup> The Viking king <a href="/wiki/Sitric_C%C3%A1ech" title="Sitric Cáech">Sihtric</a> ruled the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_York" title="Scandinavian York">Kingdom of York</a> in southern Northumbria, but <a href="/wiki/Ealdred_I_of_Bamburgh" title="Ealdred I of Bamburgh">Ealdred</a> maintained Anglo-Saxon rule in at least part of the former kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a> from his base in <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh" title="Bamburgh">Bamburgh</a> in northern Northumbria. <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Constantine II</a> ruled Scotland, apart from the southwest, which was the British <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a>. Wales was divided into a number of small kingdoms, including <a href="/wiki/Deheubarth" title="Deheubarth">Deheubarth</a> in the southwest, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwent" title="Kingdom of Gwent">Gwent</a> in the southeast, <a href="/wiki/Brycheiniog" title="Brycheiniog">Brycheiniog</a> immediately north of Gwent, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Gwynedd" title="Kingdom of Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aethelfleda_Monument,_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg"><img alt="Statue of Æthelflæd, Lady of the Mercians with Æthelstan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="634" data-file-width="386" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg/170px-Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg/255px-Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg/340px-Aethelfleda_Monument%2C_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aethelfleda_Monument,_Tamworth_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1740828.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue in Tamworth of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfl%C3%A6d" title="Æthelflæd">Æthelflæd, Lady of the Mercians</a>, with her nephew Æthelstan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a>, Æthelstan was thirty years old when he came to the throne in 924, which would mean that he was born in about 894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[10]</a></sup> He was the oldest son of Edward the Elder and also the tallest. He was Edward's only son by his first consort, <a href="/wiki/Ecgwynn" title="Ecgwynn">Ecgwynn</a>. Very little is known about Ecgwynn, and she is not named in any pre-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Conquest</a> source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNB_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNB-14">[11]</a></sup> Medieval chroniclers gave varying descriptions of her rank: one described her as an ignoble consort of inferior birth, while others described her birth as noble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup> Modern historians also disagree about her status. <a href="/wiki/Simon_Keynes" title="Simon Keynes">Simon Keynes</a> and Richard Abels believe that leading figures in Wessex were unwilling to accept Æthelstan as king in 924 partly because his mother had been Edward the Elder's concubine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Yorke" title="Barbara Yorke">Barbara Yorke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Foot" title="Sarah Foot">Sarah Foot</a> argue that allegations that Æthelstan was illegitimate were a product of the dispute over the succession, and that there is no reason to doubt that she was Edward's legitimate wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> She may have been related to <a href="/wiki/Dunstan" title="Dunstan">St Dunstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>William of Malmesbury wrote that Alfred the Great honoured his young grandson with a ceremony in which he gave him a scarlet cloak, a belt set with gems, and a sword with a gilded scabbard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_31_33_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_31_33-19">[16]</a></sup> Medieval Latin scholar <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lapidge" title="Michael Lapidge">Michael Lapidge</a> and historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wood_(historian)" title="Michael Wood (historian)">Michael Wood</a> see this as designating Æthelstan as a potential heir at a time when the claim of Alfred's nephew, Æthelwold, to the throne represented a threat to the succession of Alfred's direct line,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> but historian <a href="/wiki/Janet_Nelson" title="Janet Nelson">Janet Nelson</a> suggests that it should be seen in the context of conflict between Alfred and Edward in the 890s, and might reflect an intention to divide the realm between his son and his grandson after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> Historian Martin Ryan goes further, suggesting that at the end of his life Alfred may have favoured Æthelstan rather than Edward as his successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Acrostic" title="Acrostic">acrostic</a> poem praising prince "Adalstan", and prophesying a great future for him, has been interpreted by Lapidge as referring to the young Æthelstan, punning on the old English meaning of his name, "noble stone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> Lapidge and Wood see the poem as a commemoration of Alfred's ceremony by one of his leading scholars, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Old_Saxon" title="John the Old Saxon">John the Old Saxon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> In Michael Wood's view, the poem confirms the truth of William of Malmesbury's account of the ceremony. Wood also suggests that Æthelstan may have been the first English king to be groomed from childhood as an intellectual, and that John was probably his tutor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> However, Sarah Foot argues that the acrostic poem makes better sense if it is dated to the beginning of Æthelstan's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward married his second wife, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lffl%C3%A6d,_wife_of_Edward_the_Elder" title="Ælfflæd, wife of Edward the Elder">Ælfflæd</a>, at about the time of his father's death, probably because Ecgwynn had died, although she may have been put aside. The new marriage weakened Æthelstan's position, as his step-mother naturally favoured the interests of her own sons, Ælfweard and <a href="/wiki/Edwin,_son_of_Edward_the_Elder" title="Edwin, son of Edward the Elder">Edwin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_31_33_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_31_33-19">[16]</a></sup> By 920 Edward had taken a third wife, <a href="/wiki/Eadgifu_of_Kent" title="Eadgifu of Kent">Eadgifu</a>, probably after putting Ælfflæd aside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> Eadgifu also had two sons, the future kings Edmund and <a href="/wiki/Eadred" title="Eadred">Eadred</a>. Edward had several daughters, perhaps as many as nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan's later education was probably at the Mercian court of his aunt and uncle, Æthelflæd and Æthelred, and it is likely that the young prince gained his military training in the Mercian campaigns to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>. According to a transcript dating from 1304, in 925 Æthelstan gave a charter of privileges to <a href="/wiki/St_Oswald%27s_Priory,_Gloucester" title="St Oswald's Priory, Gloucester">St Oswald's Priory, Gloucester</a>, where his aunt and uncle were buried, "according to a pact of paternal piety which he formerly pledged with Æthelred, ealdorman of the people of the Mercians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> When Edward took direct control of Mercia after Æthelflæd's death in 918, Æthelstan may have represented his father's interests there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNB_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-DNB-14">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign">Reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_struggle_for_power">The struggle for power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The struggle for power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Edward died at <a href="/wiki/Farndon,_Cheshire" title="Farndon, Cheshire">Farndon</a> in northern Mercia on 17 July 924, and the ensuing events are unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> Ælfweard, Edward's eldest son by Ælfflæd, had ranked above Æthelstan in attesting a charter in 901, and Edward may have intended Ælfweard to be his successor as king, either of Wessex only or of the whole kingdom. If Edward had intended his realms to be divided after his death, his deposition of Ælfwynn in Mercia in 918 may have been intended to prepare the way for Æthelstan's succession as king of Mercia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> When Edward died, Æthelstan was apparently with him in Mercia, while Ælfweard was in Wessex. Mercia acknowledged Æthelstan as king, and Wessex may have chosen Ælfweard. However, Ælfweard outlived his father by only sixteen days, disrupting any succession plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even after Ælfweard's death there seems to have been opposition to Æthelstan in Wessex, particularly in Winchester, where Ælfweard was buried. At first Æthelstan behaved as a Mercian king. A charter relating to land in Derbyshire, which appears to have been issued at a time in 925 when his authority had not yet been recognised outside Mercia, was witnessed only by Mercian bishops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> In the view of historians <a href="/wiki/David_Dumville" title="David Dumville">David Dumville</a> and Janet Nelson he may have agreed not to marry or have heirs in order to gain acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> However, Sarah Foot ascribes his decision to remain unmarried to "a religiously motivated determination on chastity as a way of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[d]</a></sup></p>
<p>The coronation of Æthelstan took place on 4 September 925 at <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a>, perhaps due to its symbolic location on the border between Wessex and Mercia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot73_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foot73-38">[34]</a></sup> He was crowned by the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athelm" title="Athelm">Athelm</a>, who probably designed or organised a new <i>ordo</i> (religious order of service) in which the king wore a crown for the first time instead of a helmet. The new <i>ordo</i> was influenced by <a href="/wiki/West_Francia" title="West Francia">West Frankish</a> liturgy and in turn became one of the sources of the medieval French <i>ordo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Opposition seems to have continued even after the coronation. According to William of Malmesbury, an otherwise unknown nobleman called Alfred plotted to blind Æthelstan on account of his supposed illegitimacy, although it is unknown whether he aimed to make himself king or was acting on behalf of Edwin, Ælfweard's younger brother. Blinding would have been a sufficient disability to render Æthelstan ineligible for kingship without incurring the odium attached to murder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup> Tensions between Æthelstan and Winchester seem to have continued for some years. The <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Winchester" title="Bishop of Winchester">Bishop of Winchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frithestan" title="Frithestan">Frithestan</a>, did not attend the coronation or witness any of Æthelstan's known charters until 928. After that he witnessed fairly regularly until his resignation in 931, but was listed in a lower position than entitled by his seniority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 933 Edwin was drowned in a shipwreck in the North Sea. His cousin, <a href="/wiki/Adelolf,_Count_of_Boulogne" title="Adelolf, Count of Boulogne">Adelolf, Count of Boulogne</a>, took his body for burial at the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint_Bertin" title="Abbey of Saint Bertin">Abbey of Saint Bertin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Omer" title="Saint-Omer">Saint-Omer</a>. According to the abbey's annalist, Folcuin, who wrongly believed that Edwin had been king, he had fled England "driven by some disturbance in his kingdom". Folcuin stated that Æthelstan sent alms to the abbey for his dead brother and received monks from the abbey graciously when they came to England, although Folcuin did not realise that Æthelstan died before the monks made the journey in 944. The twelfth-century chronicler <a href="/wiki/Symeon_of_Durham" title="Symeon of Durham">Symeon of Durham</a> said that Æthelstan ordered Edwin to be drowned, but this is generally dismissed by historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[e]</a></sup> Edwin might have fled England after an unsuccessful rebellion against his brother's rule, and his death probably helped put an end to Winchester's opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_of_the_English">King of the English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: King of the English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Isles_10th_century.svg"><img alt="Map of the British Isles in the tenth century" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1569" data-file-width="990" height="460" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/British_Isles_10th_century.svg/290px-British_Isles_10th_century.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/British_Isles_10th_century.svg/435px-British_Isles_10th_century.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/British_Isles_10th_century.svg/580px-British_Isles_10th_century.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Isles_10th_century.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The British Isles in the early tenth century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward the Elder had conquered the Danish territories in Mercia and East Anglia with the assistance of Æthelflæd and her husband, but when Edward died the Danish king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sihtric_C%C3%A1ech" title="Sihtric Cáech">Sihtric</a> still ruled the Viking Kingdom of York (formerly the southern Northumbrian kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Deira" title="Deira">Deira</a>). In January 926, Æthelstan arranged for one of his sisters to marry Sihtric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[f]</a></sup> The two kings agreed not to invade each other's territories or to support each other's enemies. The following year Sihtric died, and Æthelstan seized the chance to invade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[g]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gofraid_ua_%C3%8Dmair" title="Gofraid ua Ímair">Guthfrith</a>, a cousin of Sihtric, led a fleet from <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> to try to take the throne, but Æthelstan easily prevailed. He captured York and received the submission of the Danish people. According to a southern chronicler, he "succeeded to the kingdom of the Northumbrians", and it is uncertain whether he had to fight Guthfrith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup> Southern kings had never ruled the north, and his usurpation was met with outrage by the Northumbrians, who had always resisted southern control. However, at <a href="/wiki/Eamont_Bridge" title="Eamont Bridge">Eamont</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Penrith,_Cumbria" title="Penrith, Cumbria">Penrith</a>, on 12 July 927, King Constantine of Scotland, King <a href="/wiki/Hywel_Dda" title="Hywel Dda">Hywel Dda</a> of Deheubarth, Ealdred of Bamburgh, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owen_I_of_Strathclyde" title="Owen I of Strathclyde">King Owain of Strathclyde</a> (or Morgan ap Owain of Gwent)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[h]</a></sup> accepted Æthelstan's overlordship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[46]</a></sup> His triumph led to seven years of peace in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_20_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_20-55">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whereas Æthelstan was the first English king to achieve lordship over northern Britain, he inherited his authority over the Welsh kings from his father and aunt. In the 910s Gwent acknowledged the lordship of Wessex, and Deheubarth and Gwynedd accepted that of Æthelflæd of Mercia; following Edward's takeover of Mercia, they transferred their allegiance to him. According to William of Malmesbury, after the meeting at Eamont Æthelstan summoned the Welsh kings to Hereford, where he imposed a heavy annual tribute and fixed the border between England and Wales in the Hereford area at the River Wye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[i]</a></sup> The dominant figure in Wales was Hywel Dda of Deheubarth, described by the historian of early medieval Wales <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Charles-Edwards" title="Thomas Charles-Edwards">Thomas Charles-Edwards</a> as "the firmest ally of the 'emperors of Britain' among all the kings of his day". Welsh kings attended Æthelstan's court between 928 and 935 and witnessed charters at the head of the list of laity (apart from the kings of Scotland and Strathclyde), showing that their position was regarded as superior to that of the other great men present. The alliance produced peace between Wales and England, and within Wales, lasting throughout Æthelstan's reign, though some Welsh resented the status of their rulers as under-kings, as well as the high level of tribute imposed upon them. In <i><a href="/wiki/Armes_Prydein" title="Armes Prydein">Armes Prydein Vawr</a></i> (The Great Prophecy of Britain), a Welsh poet foresaw the day when the British would rise up against their Saxon oppressors and drive them into the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to William of Malmesbury, after the Hereford meeting Æthelstan went on to expel the Cornish from <a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>, fortify its walls, and fix the Cornish boundary at the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a>. This account is regarded sceptically by historians, however, as Cornwall had been under English rule since the mid-ninth century. Thomas Charles-Edwards describes it as "an improbable story", while historian John Reuben Davies sees it as the suppression of a British revolt and the confinement of the Cornish beyond the Tamar. Æthelstan emphasised his control by establishing a new <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Cornwall" title="Bishop of Cornwall">Cornish see</a> and appointing its <a href="/wiki/Conan_of_Cornwall" title="Conan of Cornwall">first bishop</a>, but Cornwall kept its own culture and language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_(YORYM_2000_623)_obverse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2525" data-file-width="2430" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg/220px-Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg/330px-Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg/440px-Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_%28YORYM_2000_623%29_obverse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_penny_of_King_Aethestan_(YORYM_2000_623)_obverse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver penny of King Æthelstan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Æthelstan became the first king of all the Anglo-Saxon peoples, and in effect overlord of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_20_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_20-55">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[j]</a></sup> His successes inaugurated what <a href="/wiki/John_Maddicott" title="John Maddicott">John Maddicott</a>, in his history of the origins of the English Parliament, calls the <i>imperial phase</i> of English kingship between about 925 and 975, when rulers from Wales and Scotland attended the assemblies of English kings and witnessed their charters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[53]</a></sup> Æthelstan tried to reconcile the aristocracy in his new territory of Northumbria to his rule. He lavished gifts on the minsters of <a href="/wiki/Beverley_Minster" title="Beverley Minster">Beverley</a>, Chester-le-Street, and <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York</a>, emphasising his Christianity. He also purchased the vast territory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amounderness" title="Amounderness">Amounderness</a> in Lancashire, and gave it to the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, his most important lieutenant in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[k]</a></sup> But he remained a resented outsider, and the northern British kingdoms preferred to ally with the pagan Norse of Dublin. In contrast to his strong control over southern Britain, his position in the north was far more tenuous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_invasion_of_Scotland_in_934">The invasion of Scotland in 934</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The invasion of Scotland in 934">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 934 Æthelstan invaded Scotland. His reasons are unclear, and historians give alternative explanations. The death of his half-brother Edwin in 933 might have finally removed factions in Wessex opposed to his rule. Guthfrith, the Norse king of Dublin who had briefly ruled Northumbria, died in 934; any resulting insecurity among the Danes would have given Æthelstan an opportunity to stamp his authority on the north. An entry in the <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_of_Clonmacnoise" title="Annals of Clonmacnoise">Annals of Clonmacnoise</a></i>, recording the death in 934 of a ruler who was possibly Ealdred of Bamburgh, suggests another possible explanation. This points to a dispute between Æthelstan and Constantine over control of his territory. The <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> briefly recorded the expedition without explanation, but the twelfth-century chronicler <a href="/wiki/John_of_Worcester" title="John of Worcester">John of Worcester</a> stated that Constantine had broken his treaty with Æthelstan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan set out on his campaign in May 934, accompanied by four Welsh kings: Hywel Dda of Deheubarth, <a href="/wiki/Idwal_Foel" title="Idwal Foel">Idwal Foel</a> of Gwynedd, Morgan ap Owain of Gwent, and Tewdwr ap Griffri of Brycheiniog. His retinue also included eighteen bishops and thirteen earls, six of whom were Danes from eastern England. By late June or early July he had reached <a href="/wiki/Chester-le-Street" title="Chester-le-Street">Chester-le-Street</a>, where he made generous gifts to the tomb of St Cuthbert, including a <a href="/wiki/Stole_(vestment)" title="Stole (vestment)">stole</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maniple_(vestment)" title="Maniple (vestment)">maniple</a> (ecclesiastical garments) originally commissioned by his step-mother Ælfflæd as a gift to Bishop Frithestan of Winchester. The invasion was launched by land and sea. According to the twelfth-century chronicler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simeon_of_Durham" title="Simeon of Durham">Simeon of Durham</a>, his land forces ravaged as far as <a href="/wiki/Dunnottar_Castle" title="Dunnottar Castle">Dunnottar</a> in north-east Scotland, while the fleet raided <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">Caithness</a>, then probably part of the Norse kingdom of Orkney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>No battles are recorded during the campaign, and chronicles do not record its outcome. By September, however, he was back in the south of England at <a href="/wiki/Buckingham" title="Buckingham">Buckingham</a>, where Constantine witnessed a charter as <i>subregulus</i>, that is a king acknowledging Æthelstan's overlordship. In 935 a charter was attested by Constantine, Owain of Strathclyde, Hywel Dda, Idwal Foel, and Morgan ap Owain. At Christmas of the same year Owain of Strathclyde was once more at Æthelstan's court along with the Welsh kings, but Constantine was not. His return to England less than two years later would be in very different circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Battle_of_Brunanburh">The Battle of Brunanburh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Battle of Brunanburh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a></div>
<p>In 934 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olaf_III_Guthfrithson" title="Olaf III Guthfrithson">Olaf Guthfrithson</a> succeeded his father Guthfrith as the Norse King of Dublin. The alliance between the Norse and the Scots was cemented by the marriage of Olaf to Constantine's daughter. By August 937 Olaf had defeated his rivals for control of the Viking part of Ireland, and he promptly launched a bid for the former Norse kingdom of York. Individually Olaf and Constantine were too weak to oppose Æthelstan, but together they could hope to challenge the dominance of Wessex. In the autumn they joined with the Strathclyde Britons under Owain to invade England. Medieval campaigning was normally conducted in the summer, and Æthelstan could hardly have expected an invasion on such a large scale so late in the year. He seems to have been slow to react, and an old Latin poem preserved by William of Malmesbury accused him of having "languished in sluggish leisure". The allies plundered English territory while Æthelstan took his time gathering a West Saxon and Mercian army. However, Michael Wood praises his caution, arguing that unlike <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold</a> in 1066, he did not allow himself to be provoked into precipitate action. When he marched north, the Welsh did not join him, and they did not fight on either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two sides met at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh" title="Battle of Brunanburh">Battle of Brunanburh</a>, resulting in an overwhelming victory for Æthelstan, supported by his young half-brother, the future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" title="Edmund I of England">King Edmund I</a>. Olaf escaped back to Dublin with the remnant of his forces, while Constantine lost a son. The English also suffered heavy losses, including two of Æthelstan's cousins, sons of Edward the Elder's younger brother, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelweard_(son_of_Alfred)" title="Æthelweard (son of Alfred)">Æthelweard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The battle was reported in the <a href="/wiki/Annals_of_Ulster" title="Annals of Ulster">Annals of Ulster</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>A great, lamentable and horrible battle was cruelly fought between the Saxons and the Northmen, in which several thousands of Northmen, who are uncounted, fell, but their king Amlaib [Olaf], escaped with a few followers. A large number of Saxons fell on the other side, but Æthelstan, king of the Saxons, enjoyed a great victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[61]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>A generation later, the chronicler <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelweard_(historian)" title="Æthelweard (historian)">Æthelweard</a> reported that it was popularly remembered as "the great battle", and it sealed Æthelstan's posthumous reputation as "victorious because of God" (in the words of the homilist <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfric_of_Eynsham" title="Ælfric of Eynsham">Ælfric of Eynsham</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[62]</a></sup> The <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> abandoned its usual terse style in favour of a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brunanburh_(poem)" title="Battle of Brunanburh (poem)">heroic poem vaunting the great victory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[l]</a></sup> employing imperial language to present Æthelstan as ruler of an empire of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[64]</a></sup> The site of the battle is uncertain, however, and over thirty sites have been suggested, with <a href="/wiki/Bromborough" title="Bromborough">Bromborough</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Wirral_Peninsula" title="Wirral Peninsula">Wirral</a> the most favoured among historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians disagree over the significance of the battle. <a href="/wiki/Alex_Woolf" title="Alex Woolf">Alex Woolf</a> describes it as a "<a href="/wiki/Pyrrhic_victory" title="Pyrrhic victory">pyrrhic victory</a>" for Æthelstan: the campaign seems to have ended in a stalemate, his power appears to have declined, and after he died Olaf acceded to the kingdom of Northumbria without resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_P._Smyth" title="Alfred P. Smyth">Alfred Smyth</a> describes it as "the greatest battle in Anglo-Saxon history", but he also states that its consequences beyond Æthelstan's reign have been overstated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[67]</a></sup> In the view of Sarah Foot, on the other hand, it would be difficult to exaggerate the battle's importance: if the Anglo-Saxons had been defeated, their hegemony over the whole mainland of Britain would have disintegrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[68]</a></sup> According to historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_Livingston" title="Michael Livingston">Michael Livingston</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd> ... it would be no small stretch to consider the battle the moment when Englishness came of age. The men who fought and died on that field forged a political map of the future that remains with us today, arguably making the Battle at Brunanburh one of the most significant battles in the long history not just of England but of the whole of the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[69]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingship">Kingship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Kingship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Painting,_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Æthelstan with Saint John of Beverley" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg/260px-Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg/390px-Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg/520px-Painting%2C_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Painting,_Beverley_Minster_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1317269.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sixteenth-century painting in <a href="/wiki/Beverley_Minster" title="Beverley Minster">Beverley Minster</a> of Æthelstan with Saint <a href="/wiki/John_of_Beverley" title="John of Beverley">John of Beverley</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Anglo-Saxon kings ruled through <a href="/wiki/Ealdorman" title="Ealdorman">ealdormen</a>, who had the highest lay status under the king. In ninth-century Wessex they each ruled a single shire, but by the middle of the tenth they had authority over a much wider area, a change probably introduced by Æthelstan to deal with the problems of governing his extended realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[70]</a></sup> One of the ealdormen, who was also called <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan_Half-King" title="Æthelstan Half-King">Æthelstan</a>, governed the eastern Danelaw territory of East Anglia, the largest and wealthiest province of England. After the king's death, he became so powerful that he was known as Æthelstan Half-King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[71]</a></sup> Several of the ealdormen who witnessed charters had Scandinavian names, and while the localities they came from cannot be identified, they were almost certainly the successors of the earls who led Danish armies in the time of Edward the Elder, and who were retained by Æthelstan as his representatives in local government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beneath the ealdormen, reeves—royal officials who were noble local landowners—were in charge of a town or royal estate. The authority of church and state was not separated in early medieval societies, and the lay officials worked closely with their diocesan bishop and local abbots, who also attended the king's royal councils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the first king of all the Anglo-Saxon peoples, Æthelstan needed effective means to govern his extended realm. Building on the foundations of his predecessors, he created the most centralised government that England had yet seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_10_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_10-86">[74]</a></sup> Previously, some charters had been produced by royal priests and others by members of religious houses, but between 928 and 935 they were produced exclusively by a scribe known to historians as "<a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan_A" title="Æthelstan A">Æthelstan A</a>", showing an unprecedented degree of royal control over an important activity. Unlike earlier and later charters, "Æthelstan A" provides full details of the date and place of adoption and an unusually long witness list, providing crucial information for historians. After "Æthelstan A" retired or died, charters reverted to a simpler form, suggesting that they had been the work of an individual, rather than the development of a formal writing office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>A key mechanism of government was the Royal Council (or <a href="/wiki/Witenagemot" title="Witenagemot">witan</a>). Anglo-Saxon kings did not have a fixed capital city. Their courts were peripatetic, and their councils were held at varying locations around their realms. Æthelstan stayed mainly in Wessex, however, and controlled outlying areas by summoning leading figures to his councils. The small and intimate meetings that had been adequate until the enlargement of the kingdom under Edward the Elder gave way to large bodies attended by bishops, ealdormen, thegns, magnates from distant areas, and independent rulers who had submitted to his authority. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stenton" title="Frank Stenton">Frank Stenton</a> sees Æthelstan's councils as "national assemblies", which did much to break down the provincialism that was a barrier to the unification of England. John Maddicott goes further, seeing them as the start of centralised assemblies that had a defined role in English government, and Æthelstan as "the true if unwitting founder of the English parliament".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Anglo-Saxons were the first people in northern Europe to write administrative documents in the vernacular, and law codes in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> go back to <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelberht_of_Kent" title="Æthelberht of Kent">Æthelberht of Kent</a> at the beginning of the seventh century. The law code of Alfred the Great, from the end of the ninth century, was also written in the vernacular, and he expected his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ealdormen" title="Ealdormen">ealdormen</a> to learn it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[77]</a></sup> His code was strongly influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> law going back to <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> in such areas as treason, peace-keeping, organisation of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_division)" title="Hundred (county division)">hundreds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_ordeal" title="Trial by ordeal">judicial ordeal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[78]</a></sup> It remained in force throughout the tenth century, and Æthelstan's codes were built on this foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keynes_1999_471_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes_1999_471-91">[79]</a></sup> Legal codes required the approval of the king, but they were treated as guidelines which could be adapted and added to at local level, rather than a fixed canon of regulations, and customary oral law was also important in the Anglo-Saxon period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>More legal texts survive from Æthelstan's reign than from any other tenth-century English king. The earliest appear to be his tithe edict and the "Ordinance on Charities". Four legal codes were adopted at Royal Councils in the early 930s at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grately" title="Grately">Grately</a> in Hampshire, Exeter, <a href="/wiki/Faversham" title="Faversham">Faversham</a> in Kent, and Thunderfield in Surrey. Local legal texts survive from London and Kent, and one concerning <a href="/wiki/Archenfield" title="Archenfield">the 'Dunsæte'</a> on the Welsh border probably also dates to Æthelstan's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[81]</a></sup> In the view of the historian of English law, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Wormald" title="Patrick Wormald">Patrick Wormald</a>, the laws must have been written by <a href="/wiki/Wulfhelm" title="Wulfhelm">Wulfhelm</a>, who succeeded Athelm as Archbishop of Canterbury in 926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[82]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[m]</a></sup> Other historians see Wulfhelm's role as less important, giving the main credit to Æthelstan himself, although the significance placed on the ordeal as an ecclesiastical ritual shows the increased influence of the church. <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brooks" title="Nicholas Brooks">Nicholas Brooks</a> sees the role of the bishops as marking an important stage in the increasing involvement of the church in the making and enforcement of law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two earliest codes were concerned with clerical matters, and Æthelstan stated that he acted on the advice of Wulfhelm and his bishops. The first asserts the importance of paying tithes to the church. The second enforces the duty of charity on Æthelstan's reeves, specifying the amount to be given to the poor and requiring reeves to free one penal slave annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[85]</a></sup> His religious outlook is shown in a wider sacralization of the law in his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The later codes show his concern with threats to social order, especially robbery, which he regarded as the most important manifestation of social breakdown. The first of these later codes, issued at Grately, prescribed harsh penalties, including the death penalty for anyone over twelve years old caught in the act of stealing goods worth more than eight pence. This apparently had little effect, as Æthelstan admitted in the Exeter code:</p>
<dl>
<dd>I King Æthelstan, declare that I have learned that the public peace has not been kept to the extent, either of my wishes, or of the provisions laid down at Grately, and my councillors say that I have suffered this too long.</dd>
</dl>
<p>In desperation the Council tried a different strategy, offering an amnesty to thieves if they paid compensation to their victims. The problem of powerful families protecting criminal relatives was to be solved by expelling them to other parts of the realm. This strategy did not last long, and at Thunderfield Æthelstan returned to the hard line, softened by raising the minimum age for the death penalty to fifteen "because he thought it too cruel to kill so many young people and for such small crimes as he understood to be the case everywhere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[87]</a></sup> His reign saw the first introduction of the system of <a href="/wiki/Tithing" title="Tithing">tithing</a>, sworn groups of ten or more men who were jointly responsible for peace-keeping (later known as <a href="/wiki/Frankpledge" title="Frankpledge">frankpledge</a>). Sarah Foot commented that tithing and oath-taking to deal with the problem of theft had its origin in Frankia:</p>
<dl>
<dd>But the equation of theft with disloyalty to Æthelstan's person appears peculiar to him. His preoccupation with theft—tough on theft, tough on the causes of theft—finds no direct parallel in other kings' codes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[88]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Historians differ widely regarding Æthelstan's legislation. Patrick Wormald's verdict was harsh: "The hallmark of Æthelstan's law-making is the gulf dividing its exalted aspirations from his spasmodic impact." In his view, "The legislative activity of Æthelstan's reign has rightly been dubbed 'feverish'... But the extant results are, frankly, a mess."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[89]</a></sup> In the view of Simon Keynes, however, "Without any doubt the most impressive aspect of King Æthelstan's government is the vitality of his law-making", which shows him driving his officials to do their duties and insisting on respect for the law, but also demonstrates the difficulty he had in controlling a troublesome people. Keynes sees the Grately code as "an impressive piece of legislation" showing the king's determination to maintain social order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[90]</a></sup> David Pratt describes his legislation as "a deep and far-reaching reform of legal structures, no less important than developments under King Alfred two generations earlier".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coinage">Coinage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Coinage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg"><img alt="Coin of Æthelstan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="1381" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg/220px-Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg/330px-Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg/440px-Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_924-939_coin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coin of <i>Æthelstan Rex</i>, small <a href="/wiki/Cross_patt%C3%A9e" title="Cross pattée">cross pattée</a> type, London mint, moneyer Biorneard</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 970s, Æthelstan's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_Peaceful" title="Edgar the Peaceful">King Edgar</a>, reformed the monetary system to give Anglo-Saxon England the most advanced currency in Europe, with a good quality silver coinage, which was uniform and abundant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[92]</a></sup> In Æthelstan's time, however, it was far less developed, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" title="Mint (coin)">minting</a> was still organised regionally long after Æthelstan unified the country. The Grately code included a provision that there was to be only one <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(c._600_%E2%80%93_1066)" title="History of the English penny (c. 600 – 1066)">coinage</a> across the king's dominion. However, this is in a section that appears to be copied from a code of his father, and the list of towns with mints is confined to the south, including London and Kent, but not northern Wessex or other regions. Early in Æthelstan's reign, different styles of coin were issued in each region, but after he conquered York and received the submission of the other British kings, he issued a new coinage, known as the "circumscription cross" type. This advertised his newly exalted status with the inscription, "Rex Totius Britanniae". Examples were minted in Wessex, York, and English Mercia (in Mercia bearing the title "Rex Saxorum"), but not in East Anglia or the Danelaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 930s a new coinage was issued, the "crowned bust" type, with the king shown for the first time wearing a crown with three stalks. This was eventually issued in all regions apart from Mercia, which issued coins without a ruler portrait, suggesting, in Sarah Foot's view, that any Mercian affection for a West Saxon king brought up among them quickly declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foot_2011_155_156_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foot_2011_155_156-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church">Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg"><img alt="Miniature of St Matthew in gospels presented by Æthelstan to Christ Church, Canterbury" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="845" data-file-width="642" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg/220px-Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg/330px-Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg/440px-Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coronation_Gospels_Athelstan_Saint_Matthew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Miniature of St Matthew in the Carolingian gospels presented by Æthelstan to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Priory" title="Christ Church Priory">Christ Church Priory</a>, Canterbury</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Church and state maintained close relations in the Anglo-Saxon period, both socially and politically. Churchmen attended royal feasts as well as meetings of the Royal Council. During Æthelstan's reign these relations became even closer, especially as the archbishopric of Canterbury had come under West Saxon jurisdiction since Edward the Elder annexed Mercia, and Æthelstan's conquests brought the northern church under the control of a southern king for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan appointed members of his own circle to bishoprics in Wessex, possibly to counter the influence of the Bishop of Winchester, Frithestan. One of the king's mass-priests (priests employed to say Mass in his household), <a href="/wiki/Alphege_of_Wells" title="Alphege of Wells">Ælfheah</a>, became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Wells" title="Bishop of Wells">Bishop of Wells</a>, while another, <a href="/wiki/Beornstan_of_Winchester" title="Beornstan of Winchester">Beornstan</a>, succeeded Frithestan as Bishop of Winchester. Beornstan was succeeded by another member of the royal household, also called <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfheah_the_Bald" title="Ælfheah the Bald">Ælfheah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[95]</a></sup> Two of the leading figures in the later tenth-century Benedictine revival of Edgar's reign, Dunstan and <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwold_of_Winchester" title="Æthelwold of Winchester">Æthelwold</a>, served in early life at Æthelstan's court and were ordained as priests by Ælfheah of Winchester at the king's request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[96]</a></sup> According to Æthelwold's biographer, <a href="/wiki/Wulfstan_the_Cantor" title="Wulfstan the Cantor">Wulfstan</a>, "Æthelwold spent a long period in the royal palace in the king's inseparable companionship and learned much from the king's wise men that was useful and profitable to him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oda_of_Canterbury" title="Oda of Canterbury">Oda</a>, a future Archbishop of Canterbury, was also close to Æthelstan, who appointed him <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Ramsbury" title="Bishop of Ramsbury">Bishop of Ramsbury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[98]</a></sup> Oda may have been present at the battle of Brunanburh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan was a noted collector of relics, and while this was a common practice at the time, he was marked out by the scale of his collection and the refinement of its contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[100]</a></sup> The abbot of Saint Samson in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Diocese_of_Dol" title="Ancient Diocese of Dol">Dol</a> sent him some as a gift, and in his covering letter he wrote: "we know you value relics more than earthly treasure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-114">[101]</a></sup> Æthelstan was also a generous donor of manuscripts and relics to churches and monasteries. Indeed, his reputation was so great that some monastic scribes later falsely claimed that their institutions had been beneficiaries of his largesse. He was especially devoted to the cult of <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert" title="Cuthbert">St. Cuthbert</a> in Chester-le-Street, and his gifts to the community there included <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a>'s Lives of Cuthbert. He commissioned it especially to present to Chester-le Street, and out of all manuscripts he gave to a religious foundation which survive, it is the only one which was wholly written in England during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[102]</a></sup> This has a portrait of Æthelstan presenting the book to Cuthbert, the earliest surviving manuscript portrait of an English king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[103]</a></sup> In the view of Janet Nelson, his "rituals of largesse and devotion at sites of supernatural power ... enhanced royal authority and underpinned a newly united imperial realm".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ref-1_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ref-1-114">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan had a reputation for founding churches, although it is unclear how justified this is. According to late and dubious sources, these churches included minsters at <a href="/wiki/Milton_Abbas" title="Milton Abbas">Milton Abbas</a> in Dorset and <a href="/wiki/Muchelney_Abbey" title="Muchelney Abbey">Muchelney</a> in Somerset. In the view of historian John Blair, the reputation is probably well-founded, but "These waters are muddied by Æthelstan's almost folkloric reputation as a founder, which made him a favourite hero of later origin-myths."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[104]</a></sup> However, while he was a generous donor to monasteries, he did not give land for new ones or attempt to revive the ones in the north and east destroyed by Viking attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>He also sought to build ties with continental churches. <a href="/wiki/Koenwald" title="Koenwald">Cenwald</a> was a royal priest before his appointment as <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Worcester" title="Bishop of Worcester">Bishop of Worcester</a>, and in 929 he accompanied two of Æthelstan's half-sisters to the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Saxon court</a> so that the future <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto</a>, could choose one of them as his wife. Cenwald went on to make a tour of German monasteries, giving lavish gifts on Æthelstan's behalf and receiving in return promises that the monks would pray for the king and others close to him in perpetuity. England and Saxony became closer after the marriage alliance, and German names start to appear in English documents, while Cenwald kept up the contacts he had made by subsequent correspondence, helping the transmission of continental ideas about reformed monasticism to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Learning">Learning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Learning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Dice.jpg"><img alt="Gospel Dice" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="2400" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gospel_Dice.jpg/220px-Gospel_Dice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gospel_Dice.jpg/330px-Gospel_Dice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Gospel_Dice.jpg/440px-Gospel_Dice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gospel_Dice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gospel Dice, a board game played at Æthelstan's court</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2056" data-file-width="3244" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg/220px-Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg/330px-Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg/440px-Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charter_S416_written_by_%C3%86thelstan_A_in_931.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Charter S416 of Æthelstan for Wulfgar in 931, written by "Æthelstan A"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Æthelstan built on his grandfather's efforts to revive ecclesiastical scholarship, which had fallen to a low state in the second half of the ninth century. John Blair described Æthelstan's achievement as "a determined reconstruction, visible to us especially through the circulation and production of books, of the shattered ecclesiastical culture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[107]</a></sup> He was renowned in his own day for his piety and promotion of sacred learning. His interest in education, and his reputation as a collector of books and relics, attracted a cosmopolitan group of ecclesiastical scholars to his court, particularly Bretons and Irish. Æthelstan gave extensive aid to Breton clergy who had fled Brittany following its conquest by the Vikings in 919. He made a confraternity agreement with the clergy of <a href="/wiki/Dol_Cathedral" title="Dol Cathedral">Dol Cathedral</a> in Brittany, who were then in exile in central France, and they sent him the relics of Breton saints, apparently hoping for his patronage. The contacts resulted in a surge in interest in England for commemorating Breton saints. One of the most notable scholars at Æthelstan's court was <a href="/wiki/Israel_the_Grammarian" title="Israel the Grammarian">Israel the Grammarian</a>, who may have been a Breton. Israel and "a certain Frank" drew a board game called "<a href="/wiki/Alea_evangelii" title="Alea evangelii">Gospel Dice</a>" for an Irish bishop, Dub Innse, who took it home to <a href="/wiki/Bangor,_County_Down" title="Bangor, County Down">Bangor</a>. Æthelstan's court played a crucial role in the origins of the English monastic reform movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Few prose narrative sources survive from Æthelstan's reign, but it produced an abundance of poetry, much of it Norse-influenced praise of the King in grandiose terms, such as the Brunanburh poem. Sarah Foot even made a case that <a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a> may have been composed in Æthelstan's circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan's court was the centre of a revival of the elaborate <i>hermeneutic</i> style of later Latin writers, influenced by the West Saxon scholar <a href="/wiki/Aldhelm" title="Aldhelm">Aldhelm</a> (c.639–709), and by early tenth-century French monasticism. Foreign scholars at Æthelstan's court such as Israel the Grammarian were practitioners. The style was characterised by long, convoluted sentences and a predilection for rare words and neologisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[110]</a></sup> The "Æthelstan A" charters were written in <i>hermeneutic</i> Latin. In the view of Simon Keynes it is no coincidence that they first appear immediately after the king had for the first time united England under his rule, and they show a high level of intellectual attainment and a monarchy invigorated by success and adopting the trappings of a new political order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[111]</a></sup> The style influenced architects of the late tenth-century monastic reformers educated at Æthelstan's court such as Æthelwold and Dunstan, and became a hallmark of the movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[112]</a></sup> After "Æthelstan A", charters became more simple, but the <i>hermeneutic</i> style returned in the charters of Eadwig and Edgar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/W._H._Stevenson" title="W. H. Stevenson">W. H. Stevenson</a> commented in 1898:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The object of the compilers of these charters was to express their meaning by the use of the greatest possible number of words and by the choice of the most grandiloquent, bombastic words they could find. Every sentence is so overloaded by the heaping up of unnecessary words that the meaning is almost buried out of sight. The invocation with its appended clauses, opening with pompous and partly alliterative words, will proceed amongst a blaze of verbal fireworks throughout twenty lines of smallish type, and the pyrotechnic display will be maintained with equal magnificence throughout the whole charter, leaving the reader, dazzled by the glaze and blinded by the smoke, in a state of uncertainty as to the meaning of these frequently untranslatable and usually interminable sentences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[114]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>However, Michael Lapidge argues that however unpalatable the <i>hermeneutic</i> style seems to modern taste, it was an important part of late Anglo-Saxon culture, and deserves more sympathetic attention than it has received from modern historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[115]</a></sup> In the view of historian David Woodman, "Æthelstan A" should "be accorded recognition as an individual author of no little genius, a man who not only overhauled the legal form of the diploma but also had the ability to write Latin that is as enduringly fascinating as it is complex ... In many ways the diplomas of "Æthelstan A" represent the stylistic peak of the Anglo-Saxon diplomatic tradition, a fitting complement to Æthelstan's own momentous political feats and to the forging of what would become England."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_monarch">British monarch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: British monarch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg"><img alt="Æthelstan in a fifteenth-century stained glass window" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3638" data-file-width="2572" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/220px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/330px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg/440px-Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athelstan_from_All_Souls_College_Chapel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Æthelstan in a fifteenth-century stained glass window in <a href="/wiki/All_Souls_College,_Oxford" title="All Souls College, Oxford">All Souls College Chapel, Oxford</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historians frequently comment on Æthelstan's grand and extravagant titles. On his coins and charters he is described as <i>Rex totius Britanniae</i>, or "King of the whole of Britain". A gospel book he donated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Canterbury" title="Christ Church, Canterbury">Christ Church, Canterbury</a> is inscribed "Æthelstan, king of the English and ruler of the whole of Britain with a devout mind gave this book to the primatial see of Canterbury, to the church dedicated to Christ". In charters from 931 he is "king of the English, elevated by the right hand of the almighty to the throne of the whole kingdom of Britain", and in one manuscript dedication he is even styled "<a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a> et curagulus", the titles of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine emperors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[117]</a></sup> Some historians are not impressed. "Clearly", comments Alex Woolf, "King Æthelstan was a man who had pretensions," <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[118]</a></sup> while in the view of Simon Keynes, "Æthelstan A" proclaimed his master king of Britain "by wishful extension".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keynes61_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes61-132">[119]</a></sup> But according to George Molyneaux "this is to apply an anachronistic standard: tenth-century kings had a loose but real hegemony throughout the island, and their titles only appear inflated if one assumes that kingship ought to involve domination of an intensity like that seen within the English kingdom of the eleventh and later centuries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Foreign contemporaries described him in panegyrical terms. The French chronicler <a href="/wiki/Flodoard" title="Flodoard">Flodoard</a> described him as "the king from overseas", and the <i>Annals of Ulster</i> as the "pillar of the dignity of the western world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[121]</a></sup> Some historians take a similar view. Michael Wood titled an essay, "The Making of King Aethelstan's Empire: an English Charlemagne?", and described him as "the most powerful ruler that Britain had seen since the Romans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[122]</a></sup> In the view of Veronica Ortenberg, he was "the most powerful ruler in Europe" with an army that had repeatedly defeated the Vikings; continental rulers saw him as a Carolingian emperor, who "was clearly treated as the new Charlemagne". She wrote:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Wessex kings carried an aura of power and success, which made them increasingly powerful in the 920s, while most Continental houses were in military trouble and engaged in internecine warfare. While the civil wars and the Viking attacks on the Continent had spelled the end of unity of the Carolingian empire, which had already disintegrated into separate kingdoms, military success had enabled Æthelstan to triumph at home and to attempt to go beyond the reputation of a great heroic dynasty of warrior kings, in order to develop a Carolingian ideology of kingship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[123]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_relations">European relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: European relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
