<p>The West Saxon court had connections with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingians" title="Carolingians">Carolingians</a> going back to the marriage between Æthelstan's great-grandfather <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%86thelwulf_of_Wessex" title="Æthelwulf of Wessex">Æthelwulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judith_of_Flanders" title="Judith of Flanders">Judith</a>, daughter of the king of West Francia (and future Holy Roman Emperor), <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a>, as well as the marriage of Alfred the Great's daughter, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfthryth,_Countess_of_Flanders" title="Ælfthryth, Countess of Flanders">Ælfthryth</a> to Judith's son by a later marriage, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldwin_II,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Baldwin II, Count of Flanders">Baldwin II, Count of Flanders</a>. One of Æthelstan's half-sisters, <a href="/wiki/Eadgifu_of_Wessex" title="Eadgifu of Wessex">Eadgifu</a>, married <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Simple" title="Charles the Simple">Charles the Simple</a>, king of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Franks" title="West Franks">West Franks</a>, in the late 910s. He was deposed in 922, and Eadgifu sent their son, <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV_of_France" title="Louis IV of France">Louis</a> to safety in England. By Æthelstan's time the connection was well established, and his coronation was performed with the Carolingian ceremony of anointment, probably to draw a deliberate parallel between his rule and Carolingian tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[124]</a></sup> His "crowned bust" coinage of 933–938 was the first Anglo-Saxon coinage to show the king crowned, following Carolingian iconography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like his father, Æthelstan was unwilling to marry his female relatives to his own subjects, so his sisters either entered nunneries or married foreign husbands. This was one reason for his close relations with European courts, and he married several of his half-sisters to European nobles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[126]</a></sup> in what historian Sheila Sharp called "a flurry of dynastic bridal activity unequalled again until Queen Victoria's time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[127]</a></sup> Another reason lay in the common interest on both sides of the Channel in resisting the threat from the Vikings, while the rise in the power and reputation of the royal house of Wessex made marriage with an English princess more prestigious to European rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[128]</a></sup> In 926 <a href="/wiki/Hugh_the_Great" title="Hugh the Great">Hugh</a>, Duke of the Franks, sent Æthelstan's cousin, Adelolf, Count of Boulogne, on an embassy to ask for the hand of one of Æthelstan's sisters. According to William of Malmesbury, the gifts Adelolf brought included spices, jewels, many swift horses, a crown of solid gold, the sword of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>, Charlemagne's lance, and a piece of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a>. Æthelstan sent his half-sister <a href="/wiki/Eadhild" title="Eadhild">Eadhild</a> to be Hugh's wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan's most important European alliance was with the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liudolfing" title="Liudolfing">Liudolfing</a> dynasty in <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>. The Carolingian dynasty of East Francia had died out in the early tenth century, and its new Liudolfing king, <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a>, was seen by many as an <i>arriviste</i>. He needed a royal marriage for his son to establish his legitimacy, but no suitable Carolingian princesses were available. The ancient royal line of the West Saxons provided an acceptable alternative, especially as they (wrongly) claimed descent from the seventh-century king and saint, <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a>, who was venerated in Germany. In 929 or 930 Henry sent ambassadors to Æthelstan's court seeking a wife for his son, Otto, who later became Holy Roman Emperor. Æthelstan sent two of his half-sisters, and Otto chose <a href="/wiki/Eadgyth" title="Eadgyth">Eadgyth</a>. Fifty years later, Æthelweard, a descendant of Alfred the Great's older brother, addressed his Latin version of the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> to <a href="/wiki/Mathilde,_Abbess_of_Essen" title="Mathilde, Abbess of Essen">Mathilde, Abbess of Essen</a>, who was Eadgyth's granddaughter, and had apparently requested it. The other sister, whose name is uncertain, was married to a prince from near the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> who has not definitely been identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early medieval Europe, it was common for kings to act as foster-fathers for the sons of other kings. Æthelstan was known for the support he gave to dispossessed young royalty. In 936 he sent an English fleet to help his foster-son, <a href="/wiki/Alan_II,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Alan II, Duke of Brittany">Alan II, Duke of Brittany</a>, to regain his ancestral lands, which had been conquered by the Vikings. In the same year he assisted the son of his half-sister Eadgifu, Louis, to take the throne of West Francia, and in 939 he sent another fleet that unsuccessfully attempted to help Louis in a struggle with rebellious magnates. According to later Scandinavian sources, he helped another possible foster-son, <a href="/wiki/Haakon_the_Good" title="Haakon the Good">Hakon</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Harald_Fairhair" title="Harald Fairhair">Harald Fairhair</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, to reclaim his throne,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[131]</a></sup> and he was known among Norwegians as "Æthelstan the Good".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan's court was perhaps the most cosmopolitan of the Anglo-Saxon period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[133]</a></sup> The close contacts between the English and European courts ended soon after his death, but descent from the English royal house long remained a source of prestige for continental ruling families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[134]</a></sup> According to Frank Stenton in his history of the period, <i>Anglo-Saxon England</i>, "Between Offa and <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a> there is no English king who played so prominent or so sustained a part in the general affairs of Europe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1065" data-file-width="1500" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/260px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/390px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg/520px-King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King.athelstan.tomb.arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Empty fifteenth-century tomb of King Æthelstan at <a href="/wiki/Malmesbury_Abbey" title="Malmesbury Abbey">Malmesbury Abbey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Æthelstan died at <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a> on 27 October 939. His grandfather Alfred, his father Edward, and his half-brother Ælfweard had been buried at Winchester, but Æthelstan chose not to honour the city associated with opposition to his rule. By his own wish he was buried at <a href="/wiki/Malmesbury_Abbey" title="Malmesbury Abbey">Malmesbury Abbey</a>, where he had buried his cousins who died at Brunanburh. No other member of the West Saxon royal family was buried there, and according to William of Malmesbury, Æthelstan's choice reflected his devotion to the abbey and to the memory of its seventh-century abbot, Saint Aldhelm. William described Æthelstan as fair-haired "as I have seen for myself in his remains, beautifully intertwined with gold threads". His bones were lost during the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation</a>, but he is commemorated by an empty fifteenth-century tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After Æthelstan's death, the men of York immediately chose the Viking king of Dublin, Olaf Guthfrithson (or his cousin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anlaf_Cuaran" title="Anlaf Cuaran">Anlaf Cuaran</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[n]</a></sup>), as their king, and Anglo-Saxon control of the north, seemingly made safe by the victory of Brunanburh, collapsed. The reigns of Æthelstan's half-brothers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" title="Edmund I of England">Edmund</a> (939–946) and Eadred (946–955) were largely devoted to regaining control. Olaf seized the east midlands, leading to the establishment of a frontier at <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">Watling Street</a>. In 941 Olaf died, and Edmund took back control of the east midlands, and then York in 944. Following Edmund's death York again switched back to Viking control, and it was only when the Northumbrians finally drove out their Norwegian Viking king <a href="/wiki/Eric_Bloodaxe" title="Eric Bloodaxe">Eric Bloodaxe</a> in 954 and submitted to Eadred that Anglo-Saxon control of the whole of England was finally restored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Primary sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">Chronicle</a> sources for the life of Æthelstan are limited, and the first biography, by Sarah Foot, was only published in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[139]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a></i> in Æthelstan's reign is principally devoted to military events, and it is largely silent apart from recording his most important victories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumville_167_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumville_167-154">[140]</a></sup> An important source is the twelfth-century chronicle of William of Malmesbury, but historians are cautious about accepting his testimony, much of which cannot be verified from other sources. David Dumville goes so far as to dismiss William's account entirely, regarding him as a "treacherous witness" whose account is unfortunately influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[141]</a></sup> However, Sarah Foot is inclined to accept Michael Wood's argument that William's chronicle draws on a lost life of Æthelstan. She cautions, however, that we have no means of discovering how far William "improved" on the original.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Dumville's view Æthelstan has been seen by historians as a shadowy figure because of an apparent lack of source material, but he argues that the lack is more apparent than real.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[143]</a></sup> Charters, law codes, and coins throw considerable light on Æthelstan's government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[144]</a></sup> The scribe known to historians as "Æthelstan A", who was responsible for drafting all charters between 928 and 935, provides very detailed information, including signatories, dates, and locations, illuminating Æthelstan's progress around his realm. "Æthelstan A" may have been Bishop <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfwine_of_Lichfield" title="Ælfwine of Lichfield">Ælfwine of Lichfield</a>, who was close to the king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[145]</a></sup> By contrast with this extensive source of information, no charters survive from 910 to 924, a gap which historians struggle to explain, and which makes it difficult to assess the degree of continuity in personnel and the operation of government between the reigns of Edward and Æthelstan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[146]</a></sup> Historians are also paying increasing attention to less conventional sources, such as contemporary poetry in his praise and manuscripts associated with his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The reign of Æthelstan has been overshadowed by the achievements of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>, but he is now considered one of the greatest kings of the West Saxon dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkAge_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-162">[148]</a></sup> Modern historians endorse the view of twelfth century chronicler William of Malmesbury that "no one more just or more learned ever governed the kingdom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[149]</a></sup> Frank Stenton and Simon Keynes both describe him as the one Anglo-Saxon king who will bear comparison with Alfred. In Keynes's view he "has long been regarded, with good reason, as a towering figure in the landscape of the tenth century ... he has also been hailed as the first king of England, as a statesman of international standing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[150]</a></sup> David Dumville describes Æthelstan as "the father of mediaeval and modern England",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[151]</a></sup> while Michael Wood regards <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">Offa</a>, Alfred, and Æthelstan as the three greatest Anglo-Saxon kings, and Æthelstan as "one of the more important lay intellectuals in Anglo-Saxon history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan is regarded as the first King of England by modern historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[o]</a></sup> Although it was Eadred who would achieve the final unification of England by the permanent conquest of Viking York, Æthelstan's campaigns made this success possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkAge_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-162">[148]</a></sup> His nephew Edgar called himself King of the English and revived the claim to rule over all the peoples of Britain. Simon Keynes argued that "the consistent usages of Edgar's reign represent nothing less than a determined reaffirmation of the polity created by Æthelstan in the 930s".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[154]</a></sup> Historian Charles Insley, however, sees Æthelstan's hegemony as fragile: "The level of overlordship wielded by Æthelstan during the 930s over the rest of Britain was perhaps not attained again by an English king until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_I" title="Edward I">Edward I</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[155]</a></sup> George Molyneaux argues that:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The tendency of some modern historians to celebrate Æthelstan as "the first king of England" is, however, problematic, since there is little sign that that in his day the title <i>rex Anglorum</i> was closely or consistently tied to an area similar to that which we consider England. Indeed, when Æthelstan's rule was associated with any definite geographical expanse, the territory in question was usually the whole island of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[156]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Simon Keynes saw Æthelstan's law-making as his greatest achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keynes_1999_471_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keynes_1999_471-91">[79]</a></sup> His reign predates the sophisticated state of the later Anglo-Saxon period, but his creation of the most centralised government England had yet seen, with the king and his council working strategically to ensure acceptance of his authority and laws, laid the foundations on which his brothers and nephews would create one of the wealthiest and most advanced systems of government in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[157]</a></sup> Æthelstan's reign built upon his grandfather's ecclesiastical programme, consolidating the local ecclesiastical revival and laying the foundation for the monastic reform movement later in the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumville_167_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dumville_167-154">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Æthelstan's reputation was at its height when he died. According to Sarah Foot, "He found acclaim in his own day not only as a successful military leader and effective monarch but also as a man of devotion, committed to the promotion of religion and the patronage of learning." Later in the century, Æthelweard praised him as a very mighty king worthy of honour, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="Æthelred the Unready">Æthelred the Unready</a>, who named his eight sons after his predecessors, put Æthelstan first as the name of his eldest son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[158]</a></sup> Memory of Æthelstan then declined until it was revived by William of Malmesbury, who took a special interest in him as the one king who had chosen to be buried in his own house. William's account kept his memory alive, and he was praised by other medieval chroniclers. In the early sixteenth century <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">William Tyndale</a> justified his English translation of the Bible by stating that he had read that King Æthelstan had caused the Holy Scriptures to be translated into Anglo-Saxon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the sixteenth century onwards Alfred's reputation became dominant and Æthelstan largely disappeared from popular consciousness. <a href="/wiki/Sharon_Turner" title="Sharon Turner">Sharon Turner</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Anglo-Saxons" title="History of the Anglo-Saxons">History of the Anglo-Saxons</a></i>, first published between 1799 and 1805, played a crucial role in promoting Anglo-Saxon studies, and he helped to establish Brunanburh as a key battle in English history, but his treatment of Æthelstan was slight in comparison with Alfred. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> had only one paragraph on Æthelstan in his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_History_of_England" title="A Child's History of England">Child's History of England</a></i>, and although Anglo-Saxon history was a popular subject for nineteenth-century artists, and Alfred was frequently depicted in paintings at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> between 1769 and 1904, there was not one picture of Æthelstan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Michael Wood: "Among all the great rulers of British history, Æthelstan today is the forgotten man",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-176">[161]</a></sup> and in medieval historian <a href="/wiki/Ann_Williams_(historian)" title="Ann Williams (historian)">Ann Williams</a>'s view: "If Æthelstan has not had the reputation which accrued to his grandfather, the fault lies in the surviving sources; Æthelstan had no biographer, and the <i>Chronicle</i> for his reign is scanty. In his own day he was 'the roof-tree of the honour of the western world'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkAge_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-DarkAge-162">[148]</a></sup></p>
<table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 0.3em auto auto; font-size:88%; border:1px solid #aaa; width:auto; clear:none;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86thelstan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Old_English" title="Help:IPA for Old English">[æðeɫstɑn]</a></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Old_English" title="Help:IPA for Old English">[æðeɫstɑːn]</a></span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ninth-century West Saxon kings before Alfred the Great are generally described by historians as kings of Wessex or of the West Saxons. In the 880s Æthelred, Lord of the Mercians, accepted West Saxon lordship, and Alfred then adopted a new title, king of the Anglo-Saxons, representing his conception of a new polity of all the English people who were not under Viking rule. This endured until 927, when Æthelstan conquered Viking York, and adopted the title <i>rex anglorum</i> (king of the English), in recognition of his rule over the whole of England. The term "Englalonde" (England) came into use in the late tenth or early eleventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An allusion in the twelfth-century <a href="/wiki/Liber_Eliensis" title="Liber Eliensis">Liber Eliensis</a> to "Eadgyth, daughter of king Æthelstan" is probably a mistaken reference to his sister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An exception is George Molyneaux, who states that "There are, however, grounds to suspect that Æthelstan may have had a hand in the death of Ælfweard's full brother Edwin in 933".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historians generally describe her as his only full sister, but Maggie Bailey points out that this rests on the late testimony of William of Malmesbury, and the Anglo-Saxon Chronicle makes no such distinction when recording her marriage to Sihtric. William did not know her name, but traditions first recorded at <a href="/wiki/Bury" title="Bury">Bury</a> in the early twelfth century identify her as <a href="/wiki/Edith_of_Polesworth" title="Edith of Polesworth">Saint Edith of Polesworth</a>. This is considered uncertain, but it is likely that she entered a nunnery in widowhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some historians believe that Sihtric renounced his wife soon after the marriage and reverted to paganism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[41]</a></sup> while others merely state that Æthelstan took advantage of Sihtric's death to invade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup> In the view of Alex Woolf, it is unlikely that Sihtric repudiated her because Æthelstan would almost certainly have declared war on him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to William of Malmesbury it was Owain of Strathclyde who was present at Eamont, but the Anglo-Saxon Chronicle says Owain of Gwent. It could have been both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William of Malmesbury's report of the Hereford meeting is not mentioned in the first volume of the Oxford History of Wales, <i>Wales and the Britons 350–1064</i> by Thomas Charles-Edwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[49]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The situation in northern Northumbria, however, is unclear. In the view of Ann Williams, the submission of Ealdred of Bamburgh was probably nominal, and it is likely that he acknowledged Constantine as his lord, but Alex Woolf sees Ealdred as a semi-independent ruler acknowledging West Saxon authority, like Æthelred of Mercia a generation earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[52]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the view of Janet Nelson, Æthelstan had limited control over the north-west, and the donation of Amounderness in an area which had recently attracted many Scandinavian immigrants to "a powerful, but far from reliable, local potentate" was "a political gesture rather than a sign of prior control."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[54]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The Battle of Brunanburh: A Casebook</i> includes two versions of the poem and translations by Michael Livingston and Robert P. Creed, and Alex Woolf gives his own translation in <i>From Pictland to Alba</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[63]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wormald discusses the codes in detail in <i>The Making of English Law</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[83]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Historian Kevin Halloran argues that it was Anlaf Cuaran rather than Olaf Guthfrithson who became King of York after Æthelstan's death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[137]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-168"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-168">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David Dumville's chapter on Æthelstan in <i>Wessex and England</i> is headed 'Between Alfred the Great and Edgar the Peacemaker: Æthelstan, The First King of England', and the title of Sarah Foot's biography is <i>Æthelstan: The First King of England</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[153]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
